<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5e28d833-0e4d-4f08-a973-d3e53b5153cd'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

</script>

<div id="review_1369841391" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1369841391" />
    <a title="Tien" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1396651-tien"><img alt="Tien" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454187234p2/1396651.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1369841391?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tien" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tien" href="/user/show/1396651-tien">Tien</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788184007206" href="/book/show/26055673-the-automobile-club-of-egypt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1396651-tien?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1396651-tien?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1396651-tien?shelf=egypt">egypt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1369841391" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7181875136045946087">I must begin by acknowledging, once again, my penchant for Egypt. I’m interested in all things Egypt and am just fascinated by the people & culture. <i>The Automobile Club of Egypt</i> began with a very curious event followed by a really engaging middle and a non-ending.<br/><br/>Before chapter one began, the readers become witnesses to fictional characters coming to life. From chapter one onwards, we traced the origin of ‘automobile’, the Automobile Club in Egypt, and started following the lives of the Gaafar f</span>
  <span id="freeText7181875136045946087" style="display:none">I must begin by acknowledging, once again, my penchant for Egypt. I’m interested in all things Egypt and am just fascinated by the people &amp; culture. <i>The Automobile Club of Egypt</i> began with a very curious event followed by a really engaging middle and a non-ending.<br /><br />Before chapter one began, the readers become witnesses to fictional characters coming to life. From chapter one onwards, we traced the origin of ‘automobile’, the Automobile Club in Egypt, and started following the lives of the Gaafar family.<br /><br />The first few chapters were interspersed with passages on the invention of automobile by Karl Benz. I found these to be quite charming and was disappointed that there wasn’t more. I wonder at how accurate they are historically but Bertha &amp; Karl Benz were very interesting that I’m going to be looking up his biography.<br /><br />There are many perspectives in this novel –mostly of the Gaafar children especially Kamel and Saleha. From these two, we see the struggle of Egyptians in a world determined to keep them as they were (servants ever after, never master). However, as intelligent beings coming to understand their own worth, each sought for their own place in the world but not as prescribed to them.<br /><br />I am finding it hard to describe exactly what it is that drew me to the Gaafar family, each of the four children are so different in intellect and temperament that each perspective was unique. I can’t help but to sniff and roll my eyes at the eldest, Said, for all his posturing. And Mahmud’s perspectives amusing, despite all his shortcomings.<br /><br />The book didn’t feel like it ended for me… It felt like there should be more… There wasn’t a feeling of completion like the circle is still left open. I don’t know if it’s mean to be a series of some sort though there hasn’t been any mention of it online. I just feel that I need that extra chapter to relate to the very beginning of the novel for that all-rounded kind of feeling.<br /><br /><i>The Automobile Club of Egypt</i> is a fascinating tale with brilliant characters and excellent plot. It is a novel that captivates the reader despite only reading on the daily lives of Egyptians. It’s a fairly sizeable book but I didn’t have one jot of wandering mind as I was fully immersed in the story and very involved (vicariously) in these characters’ lives.<br /><br /><i>Thanks to Penguin Australia for paperback copy in exchange of honest review</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="7181875136045946087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1590089961" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1590089961" />
    <a title="DeB MaRtEnS" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9827967-deb-martens"><img alt="DeB MaRtEnS" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593294620p2/9827967.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1590089961?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DeB MaRtEnS" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DeB MaRtEnS" href="/user/show/9827967-deb-martens">DeB MaRtEnS</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781443408431" href="/book/show/25229206-the-automobile-club-of-egypt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=memorably-good">memorably-good</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=africa-mideast-asia">africa-mideast-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=aplus-2016">aplus-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=dramatic-fiction">dramatic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=books-about-books">books-about-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1590089961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15836658717356441757">The story opens in present time, with a harried author organizing his first computer printout of his newest novel; suddenly, before him stand two apparitions dressed in 1940’s apparel, Kamel and Saleha Gaafar, living breathing people from his book. They have come to add more, because, "You might have written it, but we are the ones who lived it."<br/><br/>This charming bit of magic realism introducing the novel (which we now know will be told from an unabridged, "warts and all" perspective),is reminiscent</span>
  <span id="freeText15836658717356441757" style="display:none">The story opens in present time, with a harried author organizing his first computer printout of his newest novel; suddenly, before him stand two apparitions dressed in 1940’s apparel, Kamel and Saleha Gaafar, living breathing people from his book. They have come to add more, because, "You might have written it, but we are the ones who lived it."<br /><br />This charming bit of magic realism introducing the novel (which we now know will be told from an unabridged, "warts and all" perspective),is reminiscent of much Arabian storytelling: the wise man, the sage, the mystic recounts that which he was told to be true. The device is a useful one in a myriad of ways; the teller may weave lessons for his audience or, avoid responsibility for the inherent "truths" in an unstable political situation, or simply bemuse the reader and ready them for many adventures, villains, heroes, lovers and a great tale. <br /><br />The Automobile Club of Egypt by Alaa Al Aswany fits the bill. It is an English translation of the original Arabic, written for an Egyptian public. British phrases of the era are used throughout, appropriate as the somewhat "old-fashioned" English spoken at that time. The setting is Cairo, post WWII, prior to Egypt becoming fully independent and after years of frustration with the British imposing themselves upon the country as masters and protectors. The foreign elite maintains its rigid rituals and racial bigotry, however, seeing Egyptians as useful for providing them with service - as do local royalty, especially its indulgent and frivolous King. The setting, history, culture and social attitudes seen through the eyes of this Egyptian novelist made it quite fascinating. <br /><br />The Gaafar family have made the move after their finances have taken a turn for the worse, and Abd El-Aziz, father and husband, gets a job working at the prestigious Automobile Club. His wife, three sons and daughter start new lives. They make new friends, experience poverty, grief. Life goes on.<br /><br />James Wright, a pompous Englishman who is contemptuous of Egyptians, "They're dirty, stupid, filthy, liars and thieves", is the club manager but Alku who is the palace head chamberlain has appointed himself as staff manager. <br />While obsequious to the foreign patrons and Egyptian royalty members, Alku is a tyrant with the staff, taking their tips and meting out physical punishment for any transgression. <br /><br />Indirectly or directly, The Automobile Club is the wheel around which the characters and their overlapping stories turn, as the hierarchy seeks to maintain itself, the king spends and lives ostentatiously and the workers are kept submissive. Outside, nationalism is quietly growing as resentment increases while the country remains a British protectorate. <br /><br />Because I am not Egyptian, I don't know whether the untarnished history described and ideas generated in the novel are meant to be food for thought indirectly and slyly offered up especially to youth of Egypt, who have no memory of the novel's timeline, as they live in a radicalized political regime. After reading a number of reviews which picked this book to death (I was trying to remember why I wanted to read it!), I decided there must have been some positive ones and settled back to read. <br /><br />I let myself flow with the stories, the people, the foibles, their triumphs and hopes, their struggles for dignity in the most humbling conditions and their sheer stupidity, because everyone is human. I am sorry the book came to an end. <br /><br />Recommended for those who have a broad range of historical knowledge, an appreciation of social differences in culture, recognition of any country's vision of government as unique and an enjoyment of irony. Unless so equipped, you may miss the best of the novel otherwise. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15836658717356441757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2136446167" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2136446167" />
    <a title="Tamara Agha-Jaffar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar"><img alt="Tamara Agha-Jaffar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452453836p2/51003559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2136446167?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tamara Agha-Jaffar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tamara Agha-Jaffar" href="/user/show/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar">Tamara Agha-Jaffar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307957214" href="/book/show/23995217-the-automobile-club-of-egypt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=books-i-ve-reviewed">books-i-ve-reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=books-in-translation">books-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=middle-east-and-africa">middle-east-and-africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=2017-reading-challenge">2017-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=monotheistic-religions">monotheistic-religions</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2136446167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15829174152808767545"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23995217.The_Automobile_Club_of_Egypt' title='The Automobile Club of Egypt by Alaa Al Aswany' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Automobile Club of Egypt</a> by Alaa Al Aswany, translated into English by Russell Harris, has an odd opening chapter. Two of the Gaafar children in the novel materialize and leave the author a copy of his manuscript in which they speak in their own voices. The novel then embarks on the story of Bertha and Karl Benz and the invention of the first automobile. This segues into the formation of the Automobile Club of Egypt and the fate of the Gaafar family. <br/><br/>Founded by foreigners and the Turkish ari</span>
  <span id="freeText15829174152808767545" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23995217.The_Automobile_Club_of_Egypt" title="The Automobile Club of Egypt by Alaa Al Aswany" rel="nofollow noopener">The Automobile Club of Egypt</a> by Alaa Al Aswany, translated into English by Russell Harris, has an odd opening chapter. Two of the Gaafar children in the novel materialize and leave the author a copy of his manuscript in which they speak in their own voices. The novel then embarks on the story of Bertha and Karl Benz and the invention of the first automobile. This segues into the formation of the Automobile Club of Egypt and the fate of the Gaafar family. <br /><br />Founded by foreigners and the Turkish aristocracy and under the patronage of King Fuad, the Automobile Club serves as a venue for the king and his sycophants to gamble, drink alcohol, and engage in all manner of licentious activities with a bevy of women selected specifically for that purpose. James Wright, an English man, is appointed as the club’s managing director. The employees of the club are Egyptian, and their treatment and compensation depends on where they’re situated in the hierarchy. <br /><br />Into this club enters Abd el-Aziz Gaafar, a once respected landowner who has been reduced to taking a menial job at the club to provide for his family. His untimely death leaves his bereaved wife and four children destitute. Two of his sons, Mahmud and Kamel, become employees of the club. Mahmud earns additional income by becoming a gigolo; Kamel works in the Club’s storeroom while studying for his law degree. Said, the eldest son, marries. He abandons his mother and siblings to their own devices. And after an unfortunate marriage and divorce, Saleha decides to continue with her education to fulfill her father’s dream by becoming a university professor. <br /><br />Simmering in the background is the beginnings of the political upheaval that would eventually overthrow the king and end British rule of Egypt. We see evidence of the exploitation of the workers and the poverty that forces them to tolerate inhumane treatment. James Wright’s racism rears its ugly head in his opinions of and interactions with the local population. We also see evidence of internalized racism in how the Egyptian supervisors treat those beneath them in the hierarchy. <br /><br />The novel has some strengths. In addition to the Gaafar family, we are introduced to their neighbors and the club’s other employees. The wide array of characters, their backgrounds, overlapping stories, and struggles to survive makes for interesting reading. The narrative moves forward at a rapid pace. The plot sustains the reader’s attention with most chapters ending with a mini cliffhanger that is picked up again a few chapters later. Al-Aswany skillfully alternates the narrative between first person and third person point of view. <br /><br />There were also some weaknesses. The opening chapter in which the two characters speak to the author served no purpose, did not enhance the story in any way, and could easily have been omitted. And the ending was abrupt and lacked closure. But the main weakness lay in characterization. <br /><br />Although the Gaafar children are depicted as unique individuals with differing temperaments, the portrayal of some of the remaining characters bordered on being stereotypical: the septuagenarian women desperately seeking humiliating sex with virile, young men; the too-good-to-be true young English woman who defies her father, is enamored of all things Egypt, and falls in love with an Egyptian; the racist who spews racial bigotry every chance he gets; the corrupt officials who have no qualms about torture; and the Egyptian supervisor with his internalized racism, gleeful torment of his compatriots, and obeisance to all things western. <br /><br />In spite of these weaknesses, this was an interesting novel. It sheds light on Egypt in the 1950s, portrays the political and moral corruption of the king’s court, and illustrates the panoply of ills that imperialism inflicts on a country and its indigenous population. <br /><br />Recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15829174152808767545" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2832924136" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2832924136" />
    <a title="Jan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9049164-jan"><img alt="Jan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601911811p2/9049164.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2832924136?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan" href="/user/show/9049164-jan">Jan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789044516098" href="/book/show/28467767-de-automobielclub-van-ca-ro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9049164-jan?shelf=21st-century-lit">21st-century-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9049164-jan?shelf=lit-egyptian">lit-egyptian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2832924136" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8396494455741949062">Colliding cultures, comes to my mind as a leading motive of the novel ‘The automobile club of Egypt’ by Alaa al Aswany (in Dutch ‘De automobielclub van Caïro).<br/>Egypt, until 1952, was protected, no, was a protectorate, Great Brittain ruling, and king Faruk a puppet. Within some boundaries king Faruk had his playing ground, the automobile club serving as such. And Alku, manager of staff and all of the many servants, acts like a tyrant over the personal. In this socio-political structure the main ch</span>
  <span id="freeText8396494455741949062" style="display:none">Colliding cultures, comes to my mind as a leading motive of the novel ‘The automobile club of Egypt’ by Alaa al Aswany (in Dutch ‘De automobielclub van Caïro).<br />Egypt, until 1952, was protected, no, was a protectorate, Great Brittain ruling, and king Faruk a puppet. Within some boundaries king Faruk had his playing ground, the automobile club serving as such. And Alku, manager of staff and all of the many servants, acts like a tyrant over the personal. In this socio-political structure the main characters are members of a family that had moved from the south of Egypt to its capital Cairo. Some family members get employed at the club and surroundings.<br />The novel has much ‘couleur locale’, it has action and drama, it is beginning to be a melting pot of revolutionary ideas. Towards the end there are scenes with revolutionary acts and their effects. These elements in the story brings to mind the Arab Spring at the beginning of the decade (I’m writing this in 2019), so seventy years after the time of the novel narration.<br />I don’t like the language, the style in this book so much; the way the story is told is rather soapy than literary. Nevertheless the novel is interesting because of the psychology, because of the well depicted class clash. It’s almost as if Aswany wants to tell us that, may be, the Egyptians would have been better off when the colonial attitude with its decadencies, copied by the Egyptiian royals, wouldn’t have existed. JM</span>
  <a data-text-id="8396494455741949062" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3190034739" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3190034739" />
    <a title="Karen Alkalay-Gut" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16184268-karen-alkalay-gut"><img alt="Karen Alkalay-Gut" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357557285p2/16184268.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3190034739?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen Alkalay-Gut" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen Alkalay-Gut" href="/user/show/16184268-karen-alkalay-gut">Karen Alkalay-Gut</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307957214" href="/book/show/23995217-the-automobile-club-of-egypt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3190034739" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6944754832690708226">As I read through the gripping pages of drama and conflict surrounding the Royal Automobile Club of Egypt -- old-fashioned love stories, interwoven tales of seduction and debauchery, poverty and decadent wealth, violence and submissiveness, royalty and slavery, stories that in some ways could match those of A Thousand and One Nights -- I could not help but wonder how it was that the book opened with a scene that smacked more of post-modernism than the dramatized historical drama that followed, a</span>
  <span id="freeText6944754832690708226" style="display:none">As I read through the gripping pages of drama and conflict surrounding the Royal Automobile Club of Egypt -- old-fashioned love stories, interwoven tales of seduction and debauchery, poverty and decadent wealth, violence and submissiveness, royalty and slavery, stories that in some ways could match those of A Thousand and One Nights -- I could not help but wonder how it was that the book opened with a scene that smacked more of post-modernism than the dramatized historical drama that followed, and why it continued for two more chapters with a recounting of the history of the success of the Mercedes Benz automobile by the determined wife of the German inventor. These two very different genres introducing what will emerge as a daring tale with multiple characters about life in Cairo in the 1940’s seem to make no narrative sense.<br />Al Aswany, arguably the most popular author in the Arab world today, knows how to focus on a single story when he needs to and engage a reader as he begins to spin a juicy yarn. Why should he begin at the moment he is about to print out the copy of his latest book but is interrupted by two of the characters who visit the author to help him round out the work he had thought finished? This diversion at the outset is so bizarre that the question remained with me through the reading of the narrative even though it is not referred to again.<br />Then there is the question of the development of the automobile in the next chapter. Despite all the potential for travel, excitement and progress, there will be no almost further mention of transportation in the book. Instead the narrative veers ahead to the nineteen forties, not long before the Free Officers’ revolt in 1952 which ended with the downfall of the king and the rise of Gamal Abdel Nassar. The subject of the book emerges as the life of the elite classes and the impoverished and degraded Egyptians who have become habituated to serve it. The scene is the last years of King Farouk, and the fact that his name is never mentioned only increases his influence over the economics and morality of the country. <br />Throughout the novel, each cliff-hanging chapter end left me with the hunger to know what will happen to each one of the many fascinating characters, but also the question of how will the king and the automobile bring Egypt forward into the modern age. <br />It wasn’t unreasonable to hope for an answer. Alaa Al Aswany has been a significant political writer since the publication of his revolutionary novel about contemporary moral and political corruption. The Yacoubian Building came out in 2005, a book which is said to have been an inspiration for the revolution of January 25, 2011 that brought down President Hosni Mubarak. Al Aswany was a major leader in the eleven days of demonstrations. Since then he has continued to be involved in the political future of Egypt and the Automobile Club of Egypt published in Arabic in 2013 must have been saturated in his disappointment of the election of the reactionary Moslem Brotherhood of Mohamed Morsi and filled with anger at the Egyptian people who had allowed this to happen when democracy was so close. The repressive consequences on the society may well have led to his depiction in this book of many of the Egyptian as ignorant, fearful and slavish, unable to sustain the possibilities of any revolution.<br />This is where the Automobile Club should have come in. Erected in the mid-1920s under British auspices, the building of the Royal Automobile Club still stands in Cairo on Qasr el-Nil Street in the heart of Cairo, near the king’s palace. Instead of creating a means for progress the club became representative of the domination of the British over Egyptian society, symbolizing a kind of groundless yet powerful superiority of western moral corruption and becoming the home base for the profligate behavior of the king.<br />But it is the characters who appear to the narrator at the beginning of the story who make all the difference to the way we can view the potentially oppressive and unstable consequences of revolution. The brother and sister, Kamal and Saleha, intervene in the narrator’s narrative and interject their own voices in the book, writing their own chapters, and this intervention among all the decadence and servility reveals the hope for Egypt, a hope that is now beginning to become a reality. <br />The women in The Automobile Club of Egypt do all they can to change society. Even though it is Mrs. Benz who was, as Al Aswany has pointed out, the first driver in history, there are no women members of the club. From the unjustly treated widows to the wily lover who supervises a temporary overthrow, all are amazing women but all fail in varying degrees.<br />After the 2011 revolution there was a period of darkness in Egypt, and it is not surprising that Al Aswany turned against the people whose political disinterest created the situation of the leadership of radical Islamists. His ardent protest against the treatment of women during the protests in Tahrir Square was voiced even in the pages of the Huffington Post (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.huffingtonpost.com/alaa-al-aswany/waiting-for-the-military-_b_1345140.html">https://www.huffingtonpost.com/alaa-a...</a>)<br /> But the changes that have been made since then have shown that his earlier hopes have not been entirely dashed. In the past years four female characters presented themselves as candidates for the presidency. Although none of them won, as I write this review in 2018 six women have emerged as ministers in the government of Abdel Fateh al-Sissi: Minister of Culture Enas Abdel Dayem is the latest in the growing list but there are also Minister of Social Solidarity, Ghada Wali, Minister of International Cooperation, Sahar Nasr, Minister of Immigration and Foreign Affairs of Egyptian expats, Nabila Makram, and Minister of Planning, Hala Al-Saeed. These are all women of advanced education and great abilities and are prefigured in the innocent but determined character of Saleha.<br />Saleha insists on telling her own story, and with the help of her revolutionary brother Kamal is freed from an unsuccessful and perhaps dangerous marriage and continues in her studies. From the prospect of a future of servitude and victimization she will become a powerful university professor who will be one of those helping to create a new Egypt. In an interview in 2014 in the Huffington Post Al Aswany explains how his characters make their own decisions and often surprise him with the results. In The Automobile Club of Egypt Al Aswany presented his characters as revealing the truth that he had not been fully awake to in the dark days after the Arab Spring. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6944754832690708226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1501396634" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1501396634" />
    <a title="Pep Bonet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19603487-pep-bonet"><img alt="Pep Bonet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370186143p2/19603487.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1501396634?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pep Bonet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pep Bonet" href="/user/show/19603487-pep-bonet">Pep Bonet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/27802756-the-automobile-club-of-egypt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19603487-pep-bonet?shelf=novel-la">novel-la</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19603487-pep-bonet?shelf=africana">africana</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19603487-pep-bonet?shelf=%C3%A0rab-eg">àrab-eg</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1501396634" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1092037761300254315">I came to this book with some hesitation. It is my first contact with Arabic literature, which was unknown territory to me. And I guess that still is, since al Aswany seems to be half American, or at least has an American education. As a matter of fact, I still wonder what makes a book belong to a given literature. Is it the language? Is it the nationality of the author? Or is it the subject, the environemt? Whatever.<br/><br/>The book is well written and catches the reader's attention since the beginning</span>
  <span id="freeText1092037761300254315" style="display:none">I came to this book with some hesitation. It is my first contact with Arabic literature, which was unknown territory to me. And I guess that still is, since al Aswany seems to be half American, or at least has an American education. As a matter of fact, I still wonder what makes a book belong to a given literature. Is it the language? Is it the nationality of the author? Or is it the subject, the environemt? Whatever.<br /><br />The book is well written and catches the reader's attention since the beginning. It plays extensively with several lines of action which get more or less intertwined, even if they never completely merge. Indeed, not being a thriller, the endings can stay open.<br /><br />Basically, we have here the story of a family of empoverished well-off high Egyptians living in Cairo lives which turn around the Automobile Club, symbol of the corruot dynasty and the English occupation of Egypt. While ordinary tragedies touch the members of the family, we witness the contradictions of a society, the daily tensions between old and new, between Egyptians and English, between lower class members and the ruling class, between workers and the serving staff of the Club.<br /><br />The book is written in a clue of comedy, rather than tragedy. This justifies some licences that the author takes, for instance by converting some dramatic moments into lighter situations.<br /><br />This is a book you can't stop reading, where all chapters end as they should: by reaching a climax and letting the reader willing to know the follow-up, which is all the trickier since the follow-up is not the next chapter, but the second ort third next.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1092037761300254315" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10146507815622869730">I loved this book, couldn't put it down. Had read his previous book and was eager to read this. The author follows a family in Cairo post-WWII, and before the British have relinquished their hold on Egypt. The family has lost their fortune and been forced to relocate from Upper Egypt to Cairo; the father suddenly has to struggle to support his family while maintaining his dignity. Fascinating, well written book.</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18141179343106316990">This is the first book I've read by Aswany, but it won't be the last. He brings history to life through his characters, but I was somewhat reminded of V. J. Naipaul's A House for Mr. Biswas, not in content but in style and universality. Admittedly a little slow in the setup, but worth the effort in the end.<br/></span>

  <span id="freeText9772928381386546016" style="display:none">In all honesty, I have mixed feelings about this book. <br /><br />To begin with, I got easily sucked into the storyline. It was easy to empathize with the main characters Those are always good things. I considered Al Aswany a good storyteller ever since reading The Yacoubian. <br /><br />There are a few weak spots in my perception though, mainly the beginning and the ending of the book. The story starts off with the writer (Al Aswany himself, I suppose), who is about to finish his work and then starts doubting. In my view, there is no added value to such a manoeuvre. It seemed to be out of context and if asked I would have suggested to leave this piece out!<br /> Then the ending was quite abrupt. The story seemed to be still in full flow, and once of a sudden I'm at the last chapter! Also a bit unsatisfactory: some lights in the dark but nothing resolved, really. But I have to acknowledge that might be a matter of taste</span>

<div id="review_558385283" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/558385283" />
    <a title="MiA" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10410770-mia"><img alt="MiA" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478076427p2/10410770.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/558385283?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MiA" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MiA" href="/user/show/10410770-mia">MiA</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10410770-mia?shelf=arabic">arabic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10410770-mia?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10410770-mia?shelf=doesn-t-deserve-the-hype">doesn-t-deserve-the-hype</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer558385283" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4548351074441405178">Known for his political views on Egypt's claimed "democracy" before and after the Arab Spring, the author of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/128711.The_Yacoubian_Building' title='The Yacoubian Building by Alaa Al Aswany' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Yacoubian Building</a> continues to manifest the multi-faceted corruption in politics and society. A recurrent theme in most of his work.<br/><br/>The added element here was "history". Set against the backdrop of 1940's Egypt, AlAswani pursues the projection of modern day dilemmas of moral corruption as a result of political malfeasance and social injustice. The aim itself is high and respectable b</span>
  <span id="freeText4548351074441405178" style="display:none">Known for his political views on Egypt's claimed "democracy" before and after the Arab Spring, the author of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/128711.The_Yacoubian_Building" title="The Yacoubian Building by Alaa Al Aswany" rel="nofollow noopener">The Yacoubian Building</a> continues to manifest the multi-faceted corruption in politics and society. A recurrent theme in most of his work.<br /><br />The added element here was "history". Set against the backdrop of 1940's Egypt, AlAswani pursues the projection of modern day dilemmas of moral corruption as a result of political malfeasance and social injustice. The aim itself is high and respectable but the novel failed to deliver it.<br /><br />As a devotee to AlAswani's writings in papers throughout the years, I can fairly judge him to be one of the most opinionated writers (in a good way). In his essays, he attempted many projections of the Egyptian reality in hypothetical contexts. I enjoyed these pieces immensely. Now that that being said, let me explain why this novel hasn't appealed much to me.<br /><br />In my humble opinion, a historical fiction is a matter of research rather than a matter of opinion. Historical research requires a lot of effort to incorporate in a novel. Moreover, it should occupy the very fiber of the storyline, in every aspect of it, in a way that makes the historical element indispensable to the plot. Here, AlAswani found himself an establishment that can serve as a microcosm of Egypt, and toyed with the characters in an attempt to realize the aforementioned aim. It didn't do. Subtract the historical existence of the plot, change the name of the Automobile Club to modern day's Hiltons or Marriotts, that fill the uptown Cairo scene, and it wouldn't have affected the plot one bit. Apparently, history isn't AlAswani's strongest point of narration. As a matter of fact, it did more harm than good by weighing down the plot. The narration was dragging on and on aimlessly filling 644 pages that could have been much less in size and much more to the point. The whole historical thing was an affectation. Modern day struggles imposed in bulks and bunches on the supposed "historical" context that made the story belongs neither to back then nor to right now.<br /><br />I read the book as it came out in Arabic, and was tempted to write this review as its translation into English came out and in to my field of vision at this year's Cairo book fair. The translated title caught my eye. The original title of the book translates to "The Automobile Club". The translated copy I found was titled " The Automobile Club of Egypt", which made me wonder. Why the reference to the location in the title?! Why stating the obvious?! It's like saying "yes, we admit that the plot is unclear to the whereabouts of the novel". I just liked the title less.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4548351074441405178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3291002227" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Sevim Tezel Aydın" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30353551-sevim-tezel-ayd-n"><img alt="Sevim Tezel Aydın" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601295298p2/30353551.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3291002227?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sevim Tezel Aydın" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sevim Tezel Aydın" href="/user/show/30353551-sevim-tezel-ayd-n">Sevim Tezel Aydın</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780857862211" href="/book/show/34688024-the-automobile-club-of-egypt">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3291002227" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8704099233244479522">The Automobile Club of Egypt is a moving, daring, vibrant tale. In British-occupied Egypt, just after the WWII and on the eve of the 1952 revolution, once a respected landowner Abd el-Aziz Gaafar has fallen on hard times. He has lost the family fortune and been forced to move his family to Cairo. Then he takes a menial job at the Automobile Club, a luxurious lodge for its European members, where Egyptians are only obedient servants. Gaafar struggles to support his family while maintaining his di</span>
  <span id="freeText8704099233244479522" style="display:none">The Automobile Club of Egypt is a moving, daring, vibrant tale. In British-occupied Egypt, just after the WWII and on the eve of the 1952 revolution, once a respected landowner Abd el-Aziz Gaafar has fallen on hard times. He has lost the family fortune and been forced to move his family to Cairo. Then he takes a menial job at the Automobile Club, a luxurious lodge for its European members, where Egyptians are only obedient servants. Gaafar struggles to support his family while maintaining his dignity but pride gets the better of him and he stands up for himself, he is subjected to a punishment that causes his death. Now two of his sons are obliged to work in the Club. <br />As the nation on the edge of change, both servants and masters are subsumed by social unrest, and the Egyptians of the Automobile Club face a choice: to live safely but without dignity as servants, or to risk everything and fight for their rights. <br />The Automobile Club of Egypt is an essential work of social criticism about hard work without rights, about colonialism and racism, about family life and traditions… Aswany presents a good insight and critique of the whole strata of Egyptian society from the King’s quarters to the servant’s quarters. <br />I found the women in The Automobile Club of Egypt the most fascinating. Because they are fearless, determinant and they are the power behind the change in society.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8704099233244479522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tasneemali" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tasneemali" href="/user/show/4770664-tasneemali">Tasneemali</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1886011120" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9634192283502413320">From the very first pages you find yourself falling for the story and the characters. At least, this is what happened to me. The very inviting and captivating opening and first pages lure you in and stun you like no other novel I’ve read recently. <br/><br/>Writing style: his choice of words is beautiful, the description and imagery is sharp, vivid and detailed. He’s so intricate. It felt like I’m witnessing his every single word. It was like a visual fantasy that I didn’t even work hard to imagine and cr</span>
  <span id="freeText9634192283502413320" style="display:none">From the very first pages you find yourself falling for the story and the characters. At least, this is what happened to me. The very inviting and captivating opening and first pages lure you in and stun you like no other novel I’ve read recently. <br /><br />Writing style: his choice of words is beautiful, the description and imagery is sharp, vivid and detailed. He’s so intricate. It felt like I’m witnessing his every single word. It was like a visual fantasy that I didn’t even work hard to imagine and create. He did the job brilliantly all by himself, all I had to do was to be there and get lost in the story. Something I’ve personally noticed in the novel, is that the hierarchy was portrayed vividly and was very evident throughout the pages. Obviously, reflecting the nature and the environment of that time.<br /><br />Maybe the novel climaxed too soon, or didn’t even climax. Because right in the middle of it round 200-300, the novel kind of got a bit predictable. I mean we got to know all sorts of interesting, eccentric characters there maybe. And then there were more and more details of the same characters. Don’t get me wrong I’m all about storytelling and I cherish details, it’s as important for me as the plot of the story. So all the perfectly chosen words describing and deciphering this world to me was what I’ve asked for! But it still felt like something was missing! That’s why my excitement for this novel grew less and less as I was approaching the end. I still believe though, that it’s a solid so rich in characters, story lines, places and history. I didn’t find it disappointing, cheap or that explicit !</span>
  <a data-text-id="9634192283502413320" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1448161423" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1448161423" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3948936-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340858920p2/3948936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1448161423?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3948936-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307957214" href="/book/show/23995217-the-automobile-club-of-egypt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3948936-trish?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1448161423" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3537501368630660615">This is one of the books that I found most entertaining this year. Even though I was hooked in the first chapter I found the first few chapters were a bit of a distraction and even though I am not a fan of the alternating type of leaving you hanging chapters style of writing once I had been introduced to all of the characters and saw where they fitted together I started to enjoy the story and looked forward to each character's episodes. It is set in post war Egypt during the British occupation. </span>
  <span id="freeText3537501368630660615" style="display:none">This is one of the books that I found most entertaining this year. Even though I was hooked in the first chapter I found the first few chapters were a bit of a distraction and even though I am not a fan of the alternating type of leaving you hanging chapters style of writing once I had been introduced to all of the characters and saw where they fitted together I started to enjoy the story and looked forward to each character's episodes. It is set in post war Egypt during the British occupation. So there a lots of social stratas from the British to the impoverished uneducated Egyptians. I suppose you could say the only two British characters were Mr Wright the automobile club manager and his daughter who could not be more different from each other and worlds apart in the views of Egypt and the Egyptians.<br />The story is set around the fortunes and misfortunes of Gaafar family and their relationship with the Hamama family, their work colleagues at the Automobile Club and their outer Egyptian environment. <br />Many attitudes and values pervade the book. British v Egyptian, Wealthy v Poor,Muslim v Christian Men v women, bothers v sisters parents and children. Overiding is the general political climate in Egypt at the time. Inetersting to see this against our current world views of the Middle east and its politics.<br />On the one hand a lot of these issues are about lifes struggle but there are akso moments of humour love and some general life philosphies.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3537501368630660615" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1533057280" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1533057280" />
    <a title="Kris McCracken" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4297611-kris-mccracken"><img alt="Kris McCracken" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514138951p2/4297611.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1533057280?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kris McCracken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kris McCracken" href="/user/show/4297611-kris-mccracken">Kris McCracken</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307957214" href="/book/show/23995217-the-automobile-club-of-egypt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1533057280" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12341198446699355930">An interesting tale that explores the inner life of a family in turmoil, amidst the disintegration of British imperialism and the political and moral corruption of Egypt's royal court in the early-1950s. This is a book that cuts right across the Egyptian social strata, from the King's bedchamber to the servant's quarters., and as such, it presents an interesting insight into a different world.<br/><br/>HOWEVER, I'm not sure about this translation. The dialogue is often bland and stilted, but can then veer</span>
  <span id="freeText12341198446699355930" style="display:none">An interesting tale that explores the inner life of a family in turmoil, amidst the disintegration of British imperialism and the political and moral corruption of Egypt's royal court in the early-1950s. This is a book that cuts right across the Egyptian social strata, from the King's bedchamber to the servant's quarters., and as such, it presents an interesting insight into a different world.<br /><br />HOWEVER, I'm not sure about this translation. The dialogue is often bland and stilted, but can then veer into melodramatic and overwrought. It's difficult to tell whether fault lays at the feet of the author or the translation.<br /><br />The key problem that I had was that the issues and moral choices were too stark. Characters are either wholly good or bad. There's very little in terms of subtle characterisation, and the story feels like an imagined sense of the past, rather than an exploration of a specific historical period.<br /><br />With this in mind, the book could do with a more ruthless editor, it feels about 100 pages too long, and there's no doubt that it could be trimmed down without losing much. I'd recommend giving it a miss.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12341198446699355930" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer618122996613731368">This is a wonderful tale of Egypt in the early part of the ;ast century, a country essentially under the rule of the British but with a King whose debauched life style means he cares little for his own people. The story centers around the Automobile Club, managed by a british manager, but the staff are selected and ruled over by the kings right hand man, who skims off most of their tips for himself and administers beatings to anyone who dares upset him. One employee is a man who has lost the wea</span>
  <span id="freeText618122996613731368" style="display:none">This is a wonderful tale of Egypt in the early part of the ;ast century, a country essentially under the rule of the British but with a King whose debauched life style means he cares little for his own people. The story centers around the Automobile Club, managed by a british manager, but the staff are selected and ruled over by the kings right hand man, who skims off most of their tips for himself and administers beatings to anyone who dares upset him. One employee is a man who has lost the wealth and fortune he once had and is reduced to working there in order to support his family. After a beating from the king's man he dies and leaves his family in dire straits. Helped by a eurpoean manager in the club two of the mans sons are given employment in the club. The story then follows the lives of this family and their attachment to this club. A humorous look at life in Egypt at that time but with a serious side to it as well, depicting how the egyptians had so little say in their own governance.<br />Great read, I will be looking for others by this author!</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307957214" href="/book/show/23995217-the-automobile-club-of-egypt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1772592291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10475784171416491795">I struggled with the rating for this book. I enjoyed the quirky characters and the plot overall kept my attention. But the telling of the tale seemed light-hearted for the subject matter. And the ending was far too neatly wrapped up and seemed inconsistently explored. Some characters have their happy ending. Some have endings that came out of nowhere. And we don't really have endings for others. Overall I'm glad I read it, but I wouldn't pick it up again. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2572787043" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2572787043" />
    <a title="Muthoni Muiruri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24271823-muthoni-muiruri"><img alt="Muthoni Muiruri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392098994p2/24271823.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2572787043?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Muthoni Muiruri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Muthoni Muiruri" href="/user/show/24271823-muthoni-muiruri">Muthoni Muiruri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780857862211" href="/book/show/34688024-the-automobile-club-of-egypt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2572787043" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer20591409810260836">As I was reading this book, I came to the stark realisation that I knew very little about Egypt. For instance, are Egyptians predominantly Arabs or Nubians? Was the country colonised and if so, who were the Imperial powers? Was it the Turks or the Brits or both? What were the race relations like in colonial Egypt? And what about colorism? All questions quite relevant for the book.<br/><br/>I recently attended a book club discussion on this book and a majority of the attendees seemed just as clueless as I </span>
  <span id="freeText20591409810260836" style="display:none">As I was reading this book, I came to the stark realisation that I knew very little about Egypt. For instance, are Egyptians predominantly Arabs or Nubians? Was the country colonised and if so, who were the Imperial powers? Was it the Turks or the Brits or both? What were the race relations like in colonial Egypt? And what about colorism? All questions quite relevant for the book.<br /><br />I recently attended a book club discussion on this book and a majority of the attendees seemed just as clueless as I was; so suffice it to say, the discussion was quite shallow and I didn’t draw much from it. We ended up focusing solely on which characters were likeable and which ones were not. After the meet up, I had to do quite a bit of research to understand the context of the book and the time period it is set in to be able to make sense of it all.<br /><br />The Automobile Club is a historical fictional book or political book set in Cairo during the last years of the Ottoman Empire rule. It is not expressly stated, but I could deduce that this must have been in the late 40s or early 50s. The book begins with two characters, Kamal and Saleha, accosting the author and demanding an audience to tell their version of the story, and surprisingly enough, these two characters are given their own voices in the narrative. All the other stories are told in 3rd person. Though I found this interesting, I didn’t quite understand the significance seeing that the two characters were not for me outstanding in any way. <br /><br />The rest of the story revolves around the Automobile Club, an exclusive member’s only club reserved for the Turks and Brits and other vetted foreigners. Only the upper echelon Egyptians, such as the king, were allowed membership. This in itself speaks on the political structures in Egypt at the time.<br /><br />The beating and eventual death of Abd el-Aziz Gaafar, Kamel’s father, a former wealthy land owner, now storekeeper at the Automobile club, sets in motion a chain of events that drive the rest of the narrative. The plot is tightly and neatly interlaced a lot of suspense and humour. Most chapters end in cliff hangers but luckily, he revisits in subsequent chapters to fill in the gaps. <br /><br />The development of the characters was for me the highlight of the book. Aswany took time to develop his characters. They were all so believable and real, even the villains in the books were well crafted, no exaggerations. In discussions with the Author, he did reveal that most of them such as Alku, were drawn from actual people which would explain why they felt so real.<br /><br />The book covers a multitude of themes: racism, colonialism, sex, the place of women in the struggle for self-rule, change and resistance, Religion, Social class, democracy among others. <br /><br />I would recommend this book, however, ensure you have a basic understanding of Egypt in the time period the book is set it, otherwise most of it will not make sense. Aswany does not provide historical context for the book and it upon the reader to undertake some research to figure it out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="20591409810260836" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1y7BPYS2lHwcBwQbb53anGHcVHGoc7gCA6r4TKJLnVR9Xg6YHkqSsdh09vVX82d5OgLHn2zBShJBYIo9td0InA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1b9sML5Oekwk88Pf0KYpYZSq8IQEwKMk35QfHWIHDSt/z6OVJLJ8geCAMTHoyJSEz3RjasByUTSdXm1sdZGY4w==')})
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer14324189634020860101"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/73947' rel='nofollow noopener'>علاء الأسواني</a><br/><br/>Alaa al-Aswany (Arabic: علاء الأسواني‎), Egyptian Arabic (Masri) "علاء الاسوانى" (born 1957) is an Egyptian writer, and a founding member of the political movement Kefaya.<br/><br/>Trained as a dentist in Egypt and Chicago, it took him 9 years to earn his degree from Chicago National University where he spent 17 years in his life, al-Aswany has contributed numerous articles to Egyptian newspape</span>
  <span id="freeText14324189634020860101" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/73947" rel="nofollow noopener">علاء الأسواني</a><br /><br />Alaa al-Aswany (Arabic: علاء الأسواني‎), Egyptian Arabic (Masri) "علاء الاسوانى" (born 1957) is an Egyptian writer, and a founding member of the political movement Kefaya.<br /><br />Trained as a dentist in Egypt and Chicago, it took him 9 years to earn his degree from Chicago National University where he spent 17 years in his life, al-Aswany has contributed numerous articles to Egyptian newspapers on literature, politics, and social issues. His second novel, The Yacoubian Building, an ironic depiction of modern Egyptian society, has been widely read in Egypt and throughout the Middle East. It has been translated into English, Danish, Finnish, French, Norwegian, Greek and Dutch, and was adapted into a film (2006) and a television series (2007) of the same name.<br />Chicago, a novel set in the city in which the author was educated, was published in January 2007.<br />Al-Aswany participated in the Blue Metropolis in Montreal, June 2008, and was featured in interviews with the CBC programme "Writers and Company".<br /><br />Other forms of name: Alaa al-Aswani, Alaa El Aswany, Ala Aswani, Ala El Aswani </span>
  <a data-text-id="14324189634020860101" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
